CRYOGENIC ENGINEERING:

Preface
PREFACE
This book, viz. Cryogenic Engineering: Software solutions ‚Äì Part-II, is the second in the 
series on Cryogenic Engineering: Software Solutions.
This part is being published in two volumes, viz. Cryogenic Engineering: Software 
solutions ‚Äì Part-II A and Part-II B. Part-II A contains the background theory, definitions 
and formulas and the problems solved with Engineering Equation Solver (EES). Part-II B 
contains problems solved with Mathcad. So, it is advisable that one refers to both the parts.
As with the Part-I of the series, which dealt with: Introduction and properties of cryogenic 
fluids and properties of materials at low temperatures, the focus is on the solutions of 
problems in cryogenic engineering using software such as Mathcad and Engineering Equation 
Solver (EES). Only the essential theory and summary of equations required for calculations 
are given at the beginning of the chapter.
Advantages of using computer software to solve problems are reiterated:
i)	 It helps in solving the problems fast and accurately 
ii)	Parametric analysis (what-if analysis) and graphical visualization is done very easily. 
This helps in an in-depth analysis of the problem.
iii)	Once a particular type of problem is solved, it can be used as a template and solving 
similar problems later becomes extremely easy.
iv)	In addition, one can plot the data, curve fit, write functions for various properties 
or calculations and re-use them.
v)	These possibilities create interest, curiosity and wonder in the minds of students 
and enthuse them to know more and work more.
This book, viz. Cryogenic Engineering: Software solutions ‚Äì Part-II deals with the 
liquefaction of gases.
In this book: first, a thermodynamic background for cryogenic liquefaction is given; isenthalpic 
and isentropic expansion, pay-off functions for a liquefier and thermodynamically ideal 
liquefaction system are explained.
Download free eBooks at bookboon.com

CRYOGENIC ENGINEERING: 
SOFTWARE SOLUTIONS VOL II A
7
Preface
Next, various cycles for liquefaction of gases are explained and the calculation formulas for 
the pay-off functions are given. The cycles dealt with are: simple Linde ‚Äì Hampson (L-H)
system, pre-cooled L-H system, Linde dual pressure system, Claude system, Kapitza system, 
Heylandt system, cycles for liquefaction of Neon, Hydrogen and Helium, Collin‚Äôs Helium 
liquefier etc.
Finally, various types of heat exchangers, hear transfer correlations, calculation formulas and 
graphs for analysis by LMTD and NTU methods are given. And, Second Law analysis of 
the cryogenic systems is explained briefly.
Some data on commercial liquefiers for Air, Hydrogen and Helium are also given.
Many numerical problems are solved to illustrate the ease of computer calculations using 
Engineering Equation Solver (EES) and Mathcad software.
Useful data for Nitrogen, Hydrogen, Helium, Argon, Methane, Fluorine and Oxygen 
are generated from NIST website, i.e. http://webbook.nist.gov/chemistry/fluid/.
EES has built-in functions for properties of several gases, refrigerants and fluids (including 
cryogenic fluids). Therefore, it is very convenient to use EES in these calculations.
However, Mathcad does not have built-in functions for properties of gases, refrigerants 
and fluids.
So, Mathcad Functions were written for both saturation properties and superheat gas 
properties of above mentioned important cryogenic fluids, viz. Nitrogen, Hydrogen, 
Helium, Argon, Methane, Fluorine and Oxygen. These Functions were used in solving 
problems, illustrating the ease of using Mathcad in calculations and graphing.
Several Procedures/Functions are written in EES and Mathcad to simplify the standard 
and most required calculations, which, the students, teachers, researchers and professionals 
may find very useful.
S.I. Units are used throughout this book. Wide variety of worked examples presented 
in the book should be useful for those appearing for University, AMIE and Engineering 
Services examinations.
Acknowledgements: Firstly, I would like to thank all my students, who have been an 
inspiration to me in all my academic efforts.

About the Author
ABOUT THE AUTHOR
Dr. M. Thirumaleshwar graduated in Mechanical Engineering from Karnataka Regional 
Engineering College, Surathkal, Karnataka, India, in the year 1965. He obtained M.Sc 
(cryogenics) from University of Southampton, U.K. and Ph.D. (cryogenics) from Indian 
Institute of Science, Bangalore, India.
He is a Fellow of Institution of Engineers (India), Life Member, Indian Society for Technical 
Education, and a Foundation Fellow of Indian Cryogenics Council.
He has worked in India and abroad on large projects in the areas involving heat transfer, 
fluid flow, vacuum system design, cryo-pumping etc.
He worked as Head of Cryogenics Dept. in Bhabha Atomic Research Centre (BARC), 
Bombay and Centre for Advanced Technology (CAT), Indore, from 1966 to 1992.
He worked as Guest Collaborator with Superconducting Super Collider Laboratory of 
Universities Research Association, in Dallas, USA from 1990 to 1993.
He also worked at the Institute of Cryogenics, Southampton, U.K. as a Visiting Research 
Fellow from 1993 to 1994.
He was Head of the Dept. of Mechanical Engineering, Fr. Conceicao Rodrigues Institute 
of Technology, Vashi, Navi Mumbai, India for eight years.
He also worked as Head of Dept. of Mechanical Engineering and Civil Engineering, and 
then as Principal, Vivekananda College of Engineering and Technology, Puttur (D.K.), India.
He was Professor and coordinator of Post-graduate program in the Dept. of Mechanical 
Engineering in St. Joseph Engineering College, Vamanjoor, Mangalore, India.
A book entitled ‚ÄúFundamentals of Heat and Mass Transfer‚Äù authored by him and published 
by M/s Pearson Education, India (2006) has been adopted as a Text book for third year 
engineering students by the Visweswaraya Technological University (V.T.U.), Belgaum, India.

3.1	
DEFINITIONS, STATEMENTS AND FORMULAS USED [1‚Äì9]:
3.1.1	 THERMODYNAMIC BACKGROUND: JOULE-THOMSON (J-T) OR 
ISENTHALPIC EXPANSION:
When a high pressure gas passes through a valve, capillary or restriction, its pressure drops 
and the temperature also drops, generally.
From the I Law of Thermodynamics, it can be shown that in this process the enthalpy 
remains constant.
Joule-Thomson coefficient is defined as:
Obviously, 
¬µJT > 0 to produce a temp drop as the pressure drops,
¬µJT < 0 for a temp increase as the pressure drops, and
if ¬µJT = zero, then there is no change in temp as the pressure drops through the J-T valve.
Download free eBooks at bookboon.com

14
Cryogenic liquefaction systems
Hydrogen, helium, and neon have negative J-T coefficients at ambient temperature; therefore, 
these gases have to be pre-cooled to temperatures below their respective ‚Äòmax. inversion 
temperatures‚Äô, if they have to produce cooling upon expansion in a J-T valve. Following 
graph illustrates this [7]:
Inversion curve:
Pressure vs Temp curves at constant enthalpies for a gas look as follows [1]:
Download free eBooks at bookboon.com

CRYOGENIC ENGINEERING: 
SOFTWARE SOLUTIONS VOL II A
15
Cryogenic liquefaction systems
15
Note from the above graph that constant enthalpy curves go through a maximum. Locus 
of these maximums is called ‚ÄòInversion line‚Äô (3 in fig. above) and the point where this 
inversion line cuts the y-axis (i.e P = 0) is known as ‚ÄòMax. inversion temp.‚Äô Note that 
Inversion line divides the P-T graphs in to two regions, viz.:
i)	 Region of cooling upon expansion, i.e. J-T coeff. > 0, is to the left of the Inversion 
curve (region 1 in fig. above),
ii)	Region of heating upon expansion, i.e. J-T coeff. < 0, is to the right of the Inversion 
curve (region 2 in fig. above).
And, on the Inversion curve, J-T coeff. is zero.
Also note that for cooling to occur during a J-T expansion, the initial temp must be below 
the max. inversion temp.

coefficient is always positive, i.e. isentropic expansion (say, through an expansion engine) 
will always produce a temp drop.

3.1.3	 PAY-OFF FUNCTIONS FOR A LIQUEFIER:
Three pay-off functions are defined[1]:
‚Ä¢	 Work required per unit mass of gas compressed: -W/m (J/kg)
‚Ä¢	 Work required per unit mass of gas liquefied: -W/mf (J/kg)
‚Ä¢	 Fraction liquefied, y = mf/m

22
Cryogenic liquefaction systems
Then, obviously, W/m = (W/mf) * y
Note: -ve sign in the above equations only indicates that work is going in to the system.
Another parameter, i.e. Figure of Merit (FOM) is defined to compare the same system of 
liquefier for different fluids.
FOM is defined as the theoretical minimum work required divided by the actual work 
required for the system:
FOM = Wi/W = (Wi/m) / (W/mf)
FOM has a value between 0 and 1.
In an actual liquefier/refrigerator system, following performance parameters of components 
affect the overall performance of the system:
‚Ä¢	 Compressor and expander adiabatic efficiencies
‚Ä¢	 Compressor and expander mechanical efficiencies
‚Ä¢	 Heat exchanger effectiveness
‚Ä¢	 Pressure drops and
‚Ä¢	 Heat transfer to/from the system

IDEAL SYSTEM [1]:
A thermodynamically ideal liquefier system is shown below, along with its T-s diagram.

2-f: isentropic expansion, such that all the compressed gas is liquefied after expansion

3.1.8	 CLAUDE SYSTEM [1, 9]:
Claude cycle uses an expander for cooling by isentropic expansion of the gas, which gives 
a larger temp drop than for an isenthalpic expansion, for the same pressure ratio. However, 
in the final liquefaction stage, a J-T valve has to be used since expander can not have two-
phase flow through it.

Cryogenic liquefaction systems
Here, we have:
1-2: isothermal compression (to about 40 atm)
2-3: cooling in the first heat exchanger
At point 3, approx. 60% to 80% is diverted to be expanded in an expander, and then re-
united with the return stream below the second heat exchanger.
3-4: cooling in the second heat exchanger
4-5: cooling in the third heat exchanger
5-6: expansion in J-T valve
g-1:return of the cold vapor through the three heat exchangers
In large Claude systems, the work output of expander is utilized to compress the gas; but, 
in small systems, expander work is dissipated in a brake. However, whether the expander 
work is utilized or not, does not affect the liquid yield; it affects only the compressor net 
work requirement.

58
Cryogenic liquefaction systems
3.1.15	ORTHO-PARA- HYDROGEN CONVERSION IN THE LIQUEFIER [1]:
Hydrogen exists in two molecular forms: ortho-H2 and para-H2.
In ortho-H2, the two protons of the H2 molecule possess the spin in the same direction, 
and in para-H2, the spins are in opposite directions as shown below:
Mixture of o-H2 and p-H2 at high temps is called normal hydrogen, and it has 75% o-H2 
and 25% p-H2, by volume.
Equilibrium mixture of o-H2 and p-H2 is called equilibrium hydrogen (e-H2). Concentration 
of o-H2 and p-H2 in equilibrium hydrogen depends on temp. At the normal boiling point 
of hydrogen, e-H2 has a composition of 0.21% o_H2 and 99.79% p-H2, i.e. practically 
all p-H2.
Concentration of p-H2 in equilibrium H2 at different temps is shown in following Table [1]:
Now, during the liquefaction of H2, temp falls from say, 300 K to 20.3 K, and the 
conversion of ortho-H2 to para-H2 does not occur instantaneously, but occurs in a slow 
process. However, if this conversion occurs in the LH2 storage dewar, there will be a very 
high boil-off loss, since the conversion of o-H2 to p-H2 is exothermic reaction. So, the 
method is to use a catalyst to speed up the process of conversion from o-H2 to p-H2 during 
the liquefaction itself.
Download free eBooks at bookboon.com

CRYOGENIC ENGINEERING: 
SOFTWARE SOLUTIONS VOL II A
59
Cryogenic liquefaction systems
Following is the schematic of the two possible arrangements [1]:
In the above, (a) is the single expansion valve arrangement and (b) is the double expansion 
valve arrangement. The second arrangement gives approximately 20% higher liquid 
hydrogen yields.
Catalysts used are: hydrous ferric oxide, chromic oxide on alumina particles, charcoal and 
silica gel, or nickel based catalysts. Of these, hydrous ferric oxide is the most effective.
Download free eBooks at bookboon.com

Collin‚Äôs heat exchanger:

helix wrapped in the annular spaces of the tubes. The helix is soft soldered to both sides of 
the annular space. The helix acts as a fin to extend the heat transfer surface of the annulus.

three of the tubes. High pressure stream flows in the inner passages while the return, low 
pressure stream flows in the outer passages.

Fin effectiveness, surface effectiveness and overall coeff of heat transfer:
Fins are used to extend the area of heat transfer; however, 100% of fin area is not effective 
since there is some temperature gradient along the fin due to its finite thermal conductivity.
Fin-area effectiveness for plate fins and straight fins is given by:
where
In the above,
kf = thermal conductivity of the fin material,
Œ¥ = Vf / Af = Volume of fin/Surface area of fin,
Lf = Length of fin if one end of fin is free, or one-half the fin length if the fin is attached 
at both ends.
Surface effectiveness:
If the total heat transfer area is A0, then:
A0 = Af + Ap, where Af = fin surface area, and Ap = bare (or prime) surface area.
Prime surface area is 100% effective.
Then, if the surface effectiveness is Œ∑0, we can write:
Then, surface effectiveness is given by:
Note that effective heat transfer area is: Œ∑0.A0

3.1.21	SECOND LAW ANALYSIS (OR, EXERGY ANALYSIS) [25, 26, 27]: 
Summary of definitions, statements and formulas used, essential for problem solving 
only are given below:
‚ÄòAvailability‚Äô or ‚ÄòAvailable energy‚Äô or ‚ÄòExergy‚Äô: 
For a heat engine, we know from II Law that all the heat available can not be converted to 
work, and some amount of heat is necessarily to be rejected to the sink. So, the amount of 
heat rejected is the ‚Äòunavailable part‚Äô of the energy and the Wmax obtained is the ‚Äòavailable 
part‚Äô of the energy.
Thus, by definition: ‚ÄòA system delivers the max. possible work as it undergoes a reversible 
process from the specified initial state to the state if its environment, that is, the ‚Äòdead state‚Äô. 
This is the ‚Äòuseful work potential‚Äô of the system at the specified state and is called ‚ÄòExergy‚Äô.
Exergy of heat:
For a heat engine, if heat Q is supplied at a constant source temp of TH and the environment 
is at T0, the exergy or the max. work output is:
Wmax = Q.(1- T0/TH)
i.e. Wmax = Q ‚Äì T0. Œîs, where Œîs is the change in entropy in the process.
Also, the unavailable energy (or ‚Äòanergy‚Äô) = T0 .Œîs
For a refrigerator: if heat Q is absorbed at a constant source temp of Tz and the environment 
is at T0, the exergy or the min. work required is:
Wmin = Q / [Tz / (T0 ‚Äì Tz)] = Q. [(T0 / Tz) ‚Äì 1)]
i.e. Wmin = T0. Œîs ‚Äì Q, where Œîs is the change in entropy in the process.
Exergy of Work: Exergy of work is work itself, since there is no thermodynamic restriction 
on its availability.
Exergy of Kinetic Energy and Potential Energy: Again, the exergies of K.E. and P.E. are 
the respective energies themselves.
Download free eBooks at bookboon.com

Available energy = area 1-2-3-4-1
i.e. Available energy = area 1-2-6-5-1 ‚Äì area 4-3-6-5-4
i.e. Available energy = cp. (T2 ‚Äì T1) ‚Äì T0. cp. ln(T2/T1)‚Ä¶J/kg
Note that in the above equations, temp should be in Kelvin.

We have, per unit mass:
Second Law efficiency, Œ∑II[27]: Thermal efficiency for heat engines and coefficient of 
performance for refrigerators are based on the first law of thermodynamics and referred to 
as the first-law efficiencies.
Second-law efficiency is defined based on II Law:
The second-law efficiency for a heat engine is defined as the ratio of the useful work output 
to the maximum possible work output (for work-producing device, such as turbine), or the 
ratio of the minimum work input to the actual useful work input (for work-consuming 
device, such as compressor).
For refrigerators or heat Pumps, it is defined as the ratio of the actual COP to the COP of 
reversible process. For mixing chambers, the second-law efficiency is defined as the ratio of 
the exergy recovered to the exergy supplied.
Summarizing:
Device
Second Law efficiency, Œ∑II
Heat engine
Œ∑th / Œ∑rev
Work producing device (ex: Turbine)
Wu / Wrev
Work consuming device (ex: Compressor)
Wrev / Wu
Refrigerators/Heat pumps
COP / COPrev
Mixing chambers/heat exchangers
Exergy recovered / Exergy supplied

3.2	
PROBLEMS SOLVED WITH EES:
‚ÄúProb. 3.2.1 Calculate the isentropic temp drop for different gases, expanding from 20 atm, 
300 K to 1 atm.‚Äù
Solution:
First write an EES Procedure to find out the isentropic temp drop for any fluid, when it 
expands from initial pressure Pi and temp Ti to a final pressure Pf:
$UnitSysystem SI kPa K kg kJ
PROCEDURE Tdrop_isentr(FLUID$, P_i,T_i, P_f:T_f, DELTAT_s)
‚ÄúFinds the Temp drop for isentropic expansion from Pi, Ti to final pressure Pf.‚Äù
‚ÄúInputs: FLUID$, P_i,T_i, P_f:‚Ä¶Pressures in kPa, Temp in K‚Äù
‚ÄúOutputs: T_f (K) and DELTAT_s (deg.)‚Äù
s_i:=Entropy(Fluid$,T=T_i,P=P_i) ‚Äú[kJ/kg-K]‚Äù
s_f:= s_i ‚Äú[kJ/kg-K]‚Äù
Download free eBooks at bookboon.com

CRYOGENIC ENGINEERING: 
SOFTWARE SOLUTIONS VOL II A
102
Cryogenic liquefaction systems
102
T_f: =Temperature(Fluid$,s=s_f,P=P_f) ‚Äú[K]‚Äù
DELTAT_s: = T_i ‚Äì T_f
END
‚Äú=====================================================================‚Äù
Now, use the above Procedure to find the isentropic temp drop for different fluids:
‚ÄúData:‚Äù
T_i=300 ‚Äú[K]‚Äù
P_i= 20 * 101.325 ‚Äú[kPa]‚Äù
P_f = 101.325 ‚Äú[kPa]‚Äù

CRYOGENIC ENGINEERING: 
SOFTWARE SOLUTIONS VOL II A
104
Cryogenic liquefaction systems
Solution:
First write an EES Procedure to find out the isenthalpic temp drop for any fluid, when it 
expands from initial pressure Pi and temp Ti to a final pressure Pf:
$UnitSysystem SI kPa K kg kJ
PROCEDURE Tdrop_isenthalpic(FLUID$, P_i,T_i, P_f:T_f, DELTAT_h)
‚ÄúFinds Temp drop for isenthalpic expansionfrom Pi, Ti to final pressure Pf.‚Äù
‚ÄúInputs: FLUID$, P_i,T_i, P_f:‚Ä¶Pressures in kPa, Temp in K‚Äù
‚ÄúOutputs: T_f (K) and DELTAT_s (deg.)‚Äù
h_i:=Enthalpy(Fluid$,T=T_i,P=P_i) ‚Äú[kJ/kg]‚Äù
h_f:= h_i ‚Äú[kJ/kg-K]‚Äù
T_f: =Temperature(Fluid$,h=h_f,P=P_f) ‚Äú[K]‚Äù
DELTAT_h: = T_i ‚Äì T_f
END
‚Äú======================================================================‚Äù
Now, use the above Procedure to find the isentropic temp drop for different fluids:
‚ÄúData:‚Äù
T_i=300 ‚Äú[K]‚Äù
P_i= 20 * 101.325 ‚Äú[kPa]‚Äù
P_f = 101.325 ‚Äú[kPa]‚Äù

‚ÄúProb. 3.2.8 Write an EES Function to calculate the Ideal work required for liquefaction

$UnitSysystem SI kPa K kg kJ
FUNCTION Ideal_Work(FLUID$, P_1,T_1)
‚ÄúInputs: FLUID$, P_1,T_1:‚Ä¶Pressures in kPa, Temp in K‚Äù
‚ÄúOutput: W_id (kJ/kg)‚Äù

124
Cryogenic liquefaction systems
Solution:
First, write the EES Functions:
$UnitSysystem SI kPa K kg kJ
FUNCTION Wcomp_isoth(Fluid$, T_1, P_1, P_2)
{Finds Isoth. work for a compressor, Temp in Kelvin, Pressures in kPa
Inlet pressure = P_1, Outlet pressure = P_2, Temp = T_1
 Isoth work in kJ/kg}
s_1:=Entropy(Fluid$,T=T_1,P=P_1) ‚Äú[kJ/kg-K]‚Äù
s_2:=Entropy(Fluid$,P=P_2,T=T_1) ‚Äú[kJ/kg-K]‚Äù
h_1:=Enthalpy(Fluid$,T=T_1,P=P_1) ‚Äú[kJ/kg]‚Äù
h_2:=Enthalpy(Fluid$,P=P_2,T=T_1) ‚Äú[kJ/kg]‚Äù
Wcomp_isoth:= T_1 * (s_1 ‚Äì s_2) ‚Äì (h_1 ‚Äì h_2) ‚ÄúkJ/kg‚Äù
END

$UnitSysystem SI kPa K kg kJ
FUNCTION Wcomp_act(Fluid$, T_1, P_1, P_2,eta_isoth)
{Finds actual work for a compressor, Temp in Kelvin, Pressures in kPa
Inlet pressure = P_1, Outlet pressure = P_2, Temp = T_1, Isoth. effcy = eta_isoth
Actual work in kJ/kg}
Wcomp_act= Wcomp_isoth(Fluid$, T_1, P_1, P_2) / eta_isoth ‚ÄúkJ/kg‚Äù
END

Download free eBooks at bookboon.com

eta_iso = 0.8 ‚Äú‚Ä¶Isothermal effcy.‚Äù

Actual_Work_N2 = Wcomp_act(Fluid$, T1, P1, P2, eta_iso)

PROCEDURE LindeSystem(FLUID$, P_1,P_2,T_1:y,Wperkggas,Wperkgliq, W_ideal, FOM)
‚ÄúInputs: FLUID$, P_1, P_2, T_1:‚Ä¶Pressures in kPa, Temp in K‚Äù
‚ÄúOutputs: y, fraction liquefied, Wperkggas (kJ/kg of gas compressed), Wperkgliq (kJ/kg of 
liquid), W_ideal is the min. work of liquefaction (kJ/kg), and FOM is the Figure of Merit.‚Äù
s_1:=Entropy(Fluid$,T=T_1,P=P_1) ‚Äú[kJ/kg-K]‚Äù
s_2:=Entropy(Fluid$,P=P_2,T=T_1) ‚Äú[kJ/kg-K]‚Äù
h_1:=Enthalpy(Fluid$,T=T_1,P=P_1) ‚Äú[kJ/kg]‚Äù
h_2:=Enthalpy(Fluid$,P=P_2,T=T_1) ‚Äú[kJ/kg]‚Äù
h_f:=Enthalpy(Fluid$,x=0,P=P_1) ‚Äú[kJ/kg]‚Äù
y:= (h_1 ‚Äì h_2) / (h_1 ‚Äì h_f) ‚Äú‚Ä¶fraction liquefied‚Äù

CRYOGENIC ENGINEERING: 
SOFTWARE SOLUTIONS VOL II A
143
Cryogenic liquefaction systems
Or,

First, write the EES Procedure:
PROCEDURE HX_LindeSystem(FLUID$, P1,P2,T1, epsilon_HX: y, DELTAT_warmend, 
T1_prime,Q,DELTAW_comp)
‚ÄúInputs: FLUID$, P1,P2, T1:‚Ä¶Pressures in kPa, Temp in K‚Äù
‚Äúepsilon_HX = effectiveness of HX, y = liquef. fraction (mf/m)‚Äù
‚Äúy = liquefn. fraction = mf/m where mf = mass liquefied, m = mass of gas compressed‚Äù
‚ÄúOutputs: DELTAT_warmend = temp diff at warm end of HX, T1_prime = exit temp of 
low temp stream at the warm end of HX (K)‚Äù
‚ÄúQ = heat transfer in HX (kJ/kg of gas compressed)‚Äù
‚ÄúDELTAW_comp = Additional compressor work required due to HX ineffectiveness (kJ/
kg of gas compressed)‚Äù
Tsat: = Temperature(FLUID$, P = P1, x = 0))‚Äùsat. temp at P1, (K)‚Äù
h_f:=Enthalpy(Fluid$,x=0,P=P1) ‚Äú[kJ/kg]‚Äù
h_g:=Enthalpy(Fluid$,x=1,P=P1) ‚Äú[kJ/kg]‚Äù
DELTAT_warmend: = (1 ‚Äì epsilon_HX) * (T1 ‚Äì Tsat) ‚Äútemp drop at warm end of HX (K)‚Äù
T1_prime: = T1 ‚Äì DELTAT_warmend ‚Äúexit temp of low pressure stream (K)‚Äù
h_1_prime:=Enthalpy(Fluid$,T=T1_prime,P=P1) ‚Äú[kJ/kg]‚Äù
h_1: = Enthalpy(Fluid$,T=T1,P=P1) ‚Äú[kJ/kg]‚Äù
h_2: = Enthalpy(Fluid$,T=T1,P=P2) ‚Äú[kJ/kg]‚Äù
y: = (h_1_prime ‚Äì h_2) / (h_1_prime ‚Äì h_f) ‚Äú‚Ä¶liquefn. fraction‚Äù
Q: = (1 ‚Äì y) * (h_1_prime ‚Äì h_g) ‚ÄúkJ/kg of mass compressed‚Ä¶heat transfer in HX‚Äù
Download free eBooks at bookboon.com

CRYOGENIC ENGINEERING: 
SOFTWARE SOLUTIONS VOL II A
144
Cryogenic liquefaction systems
144
DELTAW_comp: = (h_1 ‚Äì h_1_prime) ‚Äú‚Ä¶additional compr. work required due to HX 
ineffcy‚Ä¶(kJ/kg of mass compressed)‚Äù
END
‚Äú======================================================================‚Äù
(b) Now, solve the problem:
‚ÄúData:‚Äù
FLUID$ = ‚ÄòNitrogen‚Äô
P1 = 101.3 ‚ÄúkPa‚Äù
T1 = 300 ‚ÄúK‚Äù
P2=200 * 101.3 ‚ÄúkPa‚Äù
epsilon_HX = 0.9
‚ÄúCalculations:‚Äù
CALL HX_LindeSystem(FLUID$, P1,P2,T1, epsilon_HX: y, DELTAT_warmend, T1_
prime,Q,DELTAW_comp)

‚ÄúProb.3.2.19 Write an EES Function to find the liquid yield as a function of pressure (P3, 
kPa) and temp (T3, K) just before the expansion valve in a Linde ‚Äì Hampson system.
And, plot the liquid yield, y against P3 for various values of T3.‚Äù
Solution:
First, write an EES Function to find out liquid yield as a function of P3, T3 for any fluid:
FUNCTION LindeSystem_Liquid_yield_y(FLUID$, P1,P3,T3)
‚ÄúInputs: FLUID$, P1, P3, T3:‚Ä¶Pressures in kPa, Temp in K‚Äù
‚ÄúP3, T3‚Ä¶pressure and temp just before the J-T valve‚Äù
‚ÄúOutput: y, fraction liquefied‚Äù
h3:=Enthalpy(Fluid$,T=T3,P=P3) ‚Äú[kJ/kg]‚Ä¶enthalpy of fluid approaching J-T valve‚Äù
hg:=Enthalpy(Fluid$,P=P1,x=1) ‚Äú[kJ/kg]‚Ä¶enthalpy of gas exiting the liquid vessel, sat. vap.‚Äù
hf:=Enthalpy(Fluid$,x=0,P=P1) ‚Äú[kJ/kg]‚Ä¶enthalpy of liquid exiting the liquid vessel, sat. liq.‚Äù

‚ÄúProb. 3.2.20 Determine the liquid yield, work per unit mass liquefied, and the work per

and 300 K and 200 atm. (at point 2). Given: refrigerant flow rate ratio, r = 0.1. See the 
schematic fig. For the refrigerant portion, point a is at 1 atm, 300K,point 2 is at 7 atm, 
375 K, and point c is at 7 atm, 300 K. [Ref.1]
(b) Also, plot y vs r for P2 = 100, 150, 200 and 250 atm.‚Äù

157
Cryogenic liquefaction systems
Solution:
‚ÄúData:‚Äù
Fluid$ = ‚ÄòNitrogen‚Äô
P1 = 101.3 ‚ÄúkPa‚Äù
T1 = 300 ‚ÄúK‚Äù
T2 = T1
P2 = 200 * 101.3 ‚ÄúkPa‚Äù
‚ÄúFor refrigerant R-12:‚Äù
Fluidrefrig$ = ‚ÄòR12‚Äô
Prefrig_a = 101.3 ‚ÄúkPa‚Äù
Trefrig_a = 300 ‚ÄúK‚Äù
Prefrig_b = 7 * 101.3 ‚ÄúkPa‚Äù
Trefrig_b = 375 ‚ÄúK‚Äù
Prefrig_c = 7 * 101.3 ‚ÄúkPa‚Äù
Trefrig_c = 300 ‚ÄúK‚Äù
r = 0.1 ‚Äúrefrigerant flow rate fraction‚Äù
‚ÄúCalculations:‚Äù
‚ÄúNitrogen system:‚Äù
s_1 = Entropy(Fluid$,T=T1,P=P1) ‚Äú[kJ/kg-K]‚Äù
s_2 = Entropy(Fluid$,T=T1,P=P2) ‚Äú[kJ/kg-K]‚Äù
s_f =Entropy(Fluid$,P=P1,x=0) ‚Äú[kJ/kg-K]‚Äù
h_1 = Enthalpy(Fluid$,T=T1,P=P1) ‚Äú[kJ/kg]‚Äù
h_2 = Enthalpy(Fluid$,T=T2,P=P2) ‚Äú[kJ/kg]‚Äù
h_f = Enthalpy(Fluid$, x = 0,P=P1) ‚Äú[kJ/kg]‚Äù
y_simpleLH = (h_1 ‚Äì h_2) / (h_1 ‚Äì h_f) ‚Äú‚Ä¶liquef. fraction for simple L-H system‚Äù

158
Cryogenic liquefaction systems
‚ÄúFor refrig system:‚Äù
h_a = Enthalpy(Fluidrefrig$,T=Trefrig_a,P=Prefrig_a) ‚Äú[kJ/kg]‚Äù
h_b = Enthalpy(Fluidrefrig$,T=Trefrig_b,P=Prefrig_b) ‚Äú[kJ/kg]‚Äù
h_c = Enthalpy(Fluidrefrig$, T = Trefrig_c,P=Prefrig_c) ‚Äú[kJ/kg]‚Äù
‚ÄúLiquid yield:‚Äù
 y_precooledLH = (h_1 ‚Äì h_2) / (h_1 ‚Äì h_f) + r * (h_a ‚Äì h_c)/(h_1 ‚Äì h_f) ‚Äú‚Ä¶liquef. 
fraction for precooled L-H system‚Äù
‚ÄúWork per unit mass of gas compressed:‚Äù
Wbym_precooledLH = T1 * (s_1 ‚Äì s_2) ‚Äì (h_1 ‚Äì h_2) + r * (h_b ‚Äì h_a) ‚ÄúkJ/kg‚Äù
‚ÄúWork per unit mass of gas liquefied:‚Äù
Wbymf_precooledLH = Wbym_precooledLH / y_precooledLH ‚ÄúkJ/kg‚Äù
‚Äú
‚Äù
‚ÄúFor simple L-H system without pre-cooling:‚Äù
‚ÄúUse the Procedure already written:‚Äù
CALL LindeSystem(‚ÄòNitrogen‚Äô, P1,P2,T1:y_N2_LH,Wperkggas_N2_LH,Wperkgliq_N2_LH, 
W_ideal_N2, FOM_N2_LH)
‚Äú
‚Äù
‚ÄúAnd, FOM for precooled L-H system:‚Äù
FOM_precooledLH = W_ideal_N2 / Wbymf_precooledLH

s_3 =Entropy(Fluid$,P=P3,T=T3) ‚Äú[kJ/kg-K]‚Äù

h_3 = Enthalpy(Fluid$,T=T3,P=P3) ‚Äú[kJ/kg]‚Äù
h_f = Enthalpy(Fluid$, x = 0,P=P1) ‚Äú[kJ/kg]‚Äù

‚Äú‚Ä¶liquef. fraction for dual pressure L-H system‚Ä¶determines h_2‚Äù
‚ÄúWork per unit mass of gas compressed:‚Äù

h_2)) ‚ÄúkJ/kg‚Äù
‚ÄúWork per unit mass of gas liquefied:‚Äù
Wbymf_dualpressureLH = Wbym_dualpressureLH / y_dualpressureLH ‚ÄúkJ/kg‚Äù
‚Äú
‚Äù
‚ÄúFor simple L-H system without precooling:‚Äù
‚ÄúUse the Procedure already written:‚Äù
CALL LindeSystem(‚ÄòAir_ha‚Äô, P1,P3,T1:y_Air_LH,Wperkggas_Air_LH,Wperkgliq_Air_LH, 
W_ideal_Air, FOM_Air_LH)
‚Äú
‚Äù
‚ÄúAnd, FOM for dual pressure L-H system:‚Äù
FOM_dualpressureLH = W_ideal_Air / Wbymf_dualpressureLH

CRYOGENIC ENGINEERING: 
SOFTWARE SOLUTIONS VOL II A
176
Cryogenic liquefaction systems
Solution:
EES Procedure:
‚ÄúIdeal Claude for liquefaction of Air and other gases: Liquef. fraction y and compr. 
work per unit mass liquefied:‚Äù
PROCEDURE ClaudeSystem_Ideal(FLUID$, P_1,P_2, P_3, T_1,T_3,x: y,W_net_
perkggas,W_net_perkgliq, W_exp_perkggas,W_ideal, FOM)
‚ÄúInputs: FLUID$, P_1, P_2, P_3,T_1, T_3:‚Ä¶Pressures in kPa, Temp in K; x = expander 
flow rate ratio‚Äù
‚ÄúOutputs: y, fraction liquefied, W_net_perkggas (kJ/kg of gas compressed), W_net_perkgliq 
(kJ/kg of liquid)‚Äù
‚ÄúW_ideal = Ideal work reqd., FOM = Figure of Merit = W_ideal/W_net_perkgliq‚Äù
s_1:=Entropy(Fluid$,T=T_1,P=P_1) ‚Äú[kJ/kg-K]‚Äù
s_2:=Entropy(Fluid$,P=P_2,T=T_1) ‚Äú[kJ/kg-K]‚Äù
s_3:=Entropy(Fluid$,P=P_3,T=T_3) ‚Äú[kJ/kg-K]‚Äù
s_e = s_3 ‚Äú‚Ä¶entropy after isentropic expansion in expander‚Äù
h_1:=Enthalpy(Fluid$,T=T_1,P=P_1) ‚Äú[kJ/kg]‚Äù
h_2:=Enthalpy(Fluid$,P=P_2,T=T_1) ‚Äú[kJ/kg]‚Äù
h_3:=Enthalpy(Fluid$,P=P_3,T=T_3) ‚Äú[kJ/kg]‚Äù
h_e = Enthalpy(Fluid$,P=P_1,s= s_e) ‚Äú[kJ/kg]‚Äù
h_f:=Enthalpy(Fluid$,x=0,P=P_1) ‚Äú[kJ/kg]‚Äù
y:= (h_1 ‚Äì h_2) / (h_1 ‚Äì h_f) + x * ((h_3 ‚Äì h_e) / (h_1 ‚Äì h_f)) ‚Äú‚Ä¶fraction liquefied‚Äù
W_exp_perkggas = x * (h_3 ‚Äì h_e) ‚ÄúkJ/kg of gas compressed‚Äù
Download free eBooks at bookboon.com

CRYOGENIC ENGINEERING: 
SOFTWARE SOLUTIONS VOL II A
177
Cryogenic liquefaction systems
177
W_net_perkggas:=T_1 * (s_1 ‚Äì s_2) ‚Äì (h_1 ‚Äì h_2) ‚Äì x * (h_3 ‚Äì h_e) ‚Äú[kJ/kg]‚Ä¶net work 
per kg gas compressed..when the expander work is utilised in compression‚Äù
W_net_perkgliq:= W_net_perkggas / y ‚Äú[kJ/kg]‚Ä¶. net work reqd per kg liq.‚Äù
W_ideal:= Ideal_Work(FLUID$, P_1,T_1) ‚ÄúkJ/kg ‚Ä¶ideal work of liquefaction‚Äù
FOM:= W_ideal / W_net_perkgliq ‚Äú‚Ä¶ Figure of Merit‚Äù
END
‚Äú======================================================================‚Äù

eta_exp_mech = 0.94 ‚Äú‚Ä¶mech effcy of expander‚Äù
eta_comp_overall = 0.75 ‚Äú‚Ä¶overall effcy of compressor‚Äù
eta_exp_overall = eta_exp_adiab * eta_exp_mech ‚Äú‚Ä¶overall effcy of expander‚Äù
s1=Entropy(Fluid$,T=T1,P=P1) ‚Äú[kJ/kg-K]‚Äù
s2=Entropy(Fluid$,P=P2,T=T1) ‚Äú[kJ/kg-K]‚Äù
s3=Entropy(Fluid$,P=P3,T=T3) ‚Äú[kJ/kg-K]‚Äù
se = s3 ‚Äú‚Ä¶entropy after isentropic expansion in expander‚Äù
h1=Enthalpy(Fluid$,T=T1,P=P1) ‚Äú[kJ/kg]‚Äù
h2=Enthalpy(Fluid$,P=P2,T=T1) ‚Äú[kJ/kg]‚Äù
h3=Enthalpy(Fluid$,P=P3,T=T3) ‚Äú[kJ/kg]‚Äù
he = Enthalpy(Fluid$,P=P1,s= se) ‚Äú[kJ/kg]‚Äù
hf=Enthalpy(Fluid$,x=0,P=P1) ‚Äú[kJ/kg]‚Äù
y_case2= (h1 ‚Äì h2) / (h1 ‚Äì hf) + x * eta_exp_adiab * ((h3 ‚Äì he) / (h1 ‚Äì hf)) ‚Äú‚Ä¶fraction 
liquefied‚Äù
W_comp_perkgliq = (1 /( y_case2 * eta_comp_overall)) * T1 * (s1 ‚Äì s2) ‚Äì (h1 ‚Äì h2) ‚Äú[kJ/
kg]‚Ä¶compressor work per kg gas liquefied..‚Äù
W_net_perkggas_case2= (T1 * (s1 ‚Äì s2) ‚Äì (h1 ‚Äì h2)) / eta_comp_overall ‚Äì x * eta_exp_
overall * (h3 ‚Äì he) ‚Äú[kJ/kg]‚Ä¶net work per kg gas compressed‚Ä¶when the expander work 
is utilised in compression‚Äù
W_net_perkgliq_case2= W_net_perkggas_case2 / y_case2 ‚Äú[kJ/kg]‚Ä¶net work reqd per kg liq.‚Äù
W_exp_perkggas_case2 = x * eta_exp_adiab * (h3 ‚Äì he) ‚ÄúkJ/kg‚Ä¶work output of expander 
per kg of gas compressed‚Äù

CRYOGENIC ENGINEERING: 
SOFTWARE SOLUTIONS VOL II A
214
Cryogenic liquefaction systems
P1 = 101.3 ‚ÄúkPa‚Ä¶pressure of nitrogen gas‚Äù
m_dot = 0.03 ‚Äúkg/s‚Ä¶mass flow rate of nitrogen gas‚Äù
D = 0.012 ‚Äúm‚Ä¶inside dia of tube‚Äù
D_h = 0.6 ‚Äúm‚Ä¶dia of helix‚Äù
T_w = 160 ‚ÄúK‚Ä¶wall temp.‚Äù
L = 50 ‚Äúm‚Ä¶length of tube‚Äù
‚ÄúCalculations‚Äù
‚ÄúProperties of Nitrogen‚Äù
mu = VISCOSITY(Nitrogen,T=T1,P=P1) ‚Äúkg/m.s‚Ä¶viscosity of nitrogen gas‚Äù
A_c = pi * D^2 /4 ‚Äúm^2‚Ä¶cross-sectional area of tube‚Äù
G = m_dot / A_c ‚Äúkg/s.m^2‚Ä¶mass velocity‚Äù
‚ÄúReynolds No.‚Äù
N_Re = G * D / mu ‚Äú = 310865‚Ä¶Reynolds No‚Ä¶ > 3000‚Ä¶therefore, turb. flow‚Äù
‚ÄúFor turb. flow calculations, fluid props should be evaluated at mean film temp Tm = 
(Tb + Tw) / 2‚Äù
T_m = (T1 + T_w) / 2 ‚Äú K‚Ä¶mean film temp‚Äù
‚ÄúThen, we have:‚Äù
‚ÄúProperties of Nitrogen‚Äù
k_t1 = CONDUCTIVITY(Nitrogen,T=T_m,P=P1) ‚ÄúW/m.K‚Ä¶thermal conductivity 
of nitrogen‚Äù
mu1 = VISCOSITY(Nitrogen,T=T_m,P=P1) ‚Äúkg/m.s‚Ä¶viscosity of nitrogen gas‚Äù
Download free eBooks at bookboon.com

CRYOGENIC ENGINEERING: 
SOFTWARE SOLUTIONS VOL II A
215
Cryogenic liquefaction systems
cp1 = SPECHEAT(Nitrogen,T=T_m,P=P1) ‚ÄúkJ/kg.K‚Ä¶sp. heat of nitrogen gas‚Äù
rho1 = DENSITY(Nitrogen,T=T_m,P=P1) ‚Äúkg/m^3‚Ä¶density of nitrogen gas‚Äù
N_Pr1 = mu1 * cp1 * 10^3 / k_t1 ‚Äú‚Ä¶Prandtl No.‚Äù
‚ÄúAnd:‚Äù
N_Re1 = G * D / mu1
‚ÄúColburn J factor:‚Äù
j_H = 0.023 * (N_Re1^ (-0.2)) * (1 + 3.5 * D / D_h) ‚Äú‚Ä¶finds Colburn j factor‚Äú
j_H = (h_c /( cp1 * 10^3 * G)) * N_Pr1^(2/3) ‚Äú‚Ä¶finds h_c, the film coeff of heat transfer, 
W/m^2.K‚Äù
‚ÄúFriction factor:‚Äù
‚ÄúNote that Reynolds no. is > 5000. So, the friction factor relation is:‚Äù
f = 0.184 * N_Re^(-0.2) ‚Äú‚Ä¶friction factor‚Äù
Results:
Thus:
Heat transfer coeff. h_c = 660.7 W/m^2.K‚Ä¶Ans.
Friction factor, f = 0.01462‚Ä¶Ans.

‚ÄúProb. 3.2.32 A circular tube is constructed of copper (kt = 50 W/m.K), has circular pin 
fins protruding from its outer surface. The circular tube has an outer dia of 25 mm. The 
pin fins are 1.25 mm in dia and 12.5 mm long. There are 2000 fins/m length of tube, and 
the fins are also of copper. The outside heat transfer coeff is 35 W/m^2.K and the tube 
temp is 100 K. Determine the fin effectiveness and the overall surface effectiveness.[1]. ‚Äú
EES Solution:
‚ÄúData:‚Äù
D_o = 0.025 ‚Äúm‚Ä¶OD of tube‚Äù
L = 1 ‚Äúm‚Ä¶length of tube‚Äù
d_f = 0.00125 ‚Äúm‚Ä¶dia of fin‚Äù
L_f = 0.0125 ‚Äúm‚Ä¶length of fin‚Äù
N_f = 2000 ‚Äú‚Ä¶no. of fins per meter length of tube‚Äù
k_f = 50 ‚ÄúW/m-K‚Ä¶thermal cond. of fin material, copper‚Äù
h_o = 35 ‚ÄúW/m^2-K‚Ä¶outside heat transfer coeff.‚Äù
‚ÄúCalculations:‚Äù
A_c = pi * d_f^2 / 4 ‚Äúm^2‚Ä¶cross-sectional area of fin‚Äù
V_f = A_c * L_f ‚Äúm^3‚Ä¶volume of fin‚Äù
A_sf = pi * d_f * L_f ‚Äúm^2‚Ä¶surface area of one fin‚Äù
‚ÄúTherefore, to find fin parameter M‚Äù
delta = V_f / A_sf ‚Äúm‚Ä¶ parameter delta to calculate fin parameter M‚Äù
M = sqrt(h_o / (k_f * delta)) ‚Äú 1/m‚Ä¶fin parameter‚Äù
Download free eBooks at bookboon.com

‚ÄúFin effectiveness eta_f:‚Äù
eta_f = tanh(M * L_f) / (M * L_f) ‚Äú‚Ä¶ fin effcy.‚Äù
{

perimeter of fin‚Äù
}
‚ÄúTo calculate Overall surface area effectiveness:‚Äù
‚ÄúTotal fin surface area:‚Äù
A_f = A_sf * N_f ‚Äúm^2‚Ä¶‚Äù

‚ÄúTotal prime area:‚Äù
A_p = L * pi * D_o ‚Äì N_f * pi * d_f^2 / 4 ‚Äúm^2‚Ä¶prime area‚Äù
‚ÄúThen, Overall surface area effectiveness:‚Äù
A_o = A_f + A_p ‚Äúm^2‚Ä¶Sum of total fin area and prime area‚Äù
eta_o = 1 ‚Äì (A_f / A_o) * (1 ‚Äì eta_f) ‚Äú‚Ä¶overall surface area effectiveness‚Äù
Results:
Thus:
Fin effectiveness = eta_f = 0.8976‚Ä¶Ans.
Overall surface area effectiveness = eta_o = 0.9423‚Ä¶Ans.

on the outside‚Äù

A_oc = pi * D_i * L ‚Äúm^2‚Ä¶inside area of heat transfer‚Äù

‚ÄúOverall heat transfer coeff. U:‚Äù

‚ÄúFluid: Helium‚Äù
P1 = 3040 ‚ÄúkPa‚Ä¶pressure of helium gas‚Äù
T_i = 300 ‚ÄúK‚Ä¶ inlet temp of helium gas‚Äù
T_o = 100 ‚ÄúK‚Ä¶exit temp of helium gas‚Äù

k_f = 150 ‚ÄúW/m.K‚Ä¶thermal cond. of fin material, i.e. Aluminium‚Äù
‚ÄúCalculations:‚Äù
A_c_f = L * t_f ‚Äúm^2‚Ä¶cross-sectional area of fin‚Äù
G = m_dot / A_ff ‚Äúkg/s.m^2‚Ä¶mass velocity‚Äù
T_b = (T_i + T_o ) / 2 ‚ÄúK‚Ä¶mean bulk temp of fluid‚Äù
‚ÄúProperties of helium gas at T_b:‚Äù
rho=Density(Helium,T=T_b,P=P1) ‚Äúkg/ m^3‚Ä¶density of helium gas‚Äù
cp=Cp(Helium,T=T_b,P=P1) ‚ÄúkJ/kg.K‚Ä¶sp. heat of helium gas‚Äù

‚ÄúReynolds No‚Ä¶etc.:‚Äù
N_Re = G * D_e /mu ‚Äú‚Ä¶Reynolds No.‚Äù
N_Pr = mu * cp * 1000/ k ‚Äú‚Ä¶Prandtl No.‚Äù
N_St = h_c / (G * cp * 1000) ‚Äú‚Ä¶Stanton No.‚Äù

(b) Gaseous nitrogen enters the warm end of a counterflow HX at 20.27 MPa and 295 K at 
a mass flow rate of 1.25 kg/s. Gaseous nitrogen enters at the cold end of the HX at 80 K. 
For the warm stream, the sp. heat is 1.296 J/g.K and the sp. heat for the cold stream is 
1.08 J/g.K. The mass flow rate of cold stream is 1.125 kg/s. The overall heat transfer coeff 
is 150 W/m^2.K and the heat transfer area on which U is based is 72.9 m^2. Determine 
the heat exchanger effectiveness, exit temp of cold stream and the heat transfer rate.[1].‚Äù
EES Procedure:
PROCEDURE CounterflowHX (m_c, m_h, cp_c, cp_h, Th_1,Tc_1,A, U: C_h, C_c, C_R, 
NTU, epsilon,Th_2, Tc_2, Q)
‚ÄúFinds the NTU, Effectiveness, heat transferred etc when the inlet temps and mass flow 
rates and sp. heats of hot and cold streams are known.‚Äù
‚ÄúInputs:: m_c, m_h, cp_c, cp_h, Th_1,Tc_1,A, U‚Ä¶mass in kg/s, cp in J/kg.KTemp in K; 
A in m^2, U in W/m^2.K‚Äù 
‚ÄúOutputs: C_h, C_c, ‚Ä¶in W/K, C_R = C_min/C_max, NTU‚Ä¶is a number‚Äù
C_h: =m_h * cp_h ‚Äú‚Ä¶capacity rate of hot fluid, W/K‚Äù
C_c: =m_c * cp_c ‚Äú‚Ä¶capacity rate of cold fluid, W/K‚Äù
IF C_c < C_h THEN
C_min: = C_c
C_max: = C_h
NTU: = (U * A) /C_min ‚Äú‚Ä¶finds NTU‚Äù
C_R: = C_min / C_max ‚Äú‚Ä¶finds capacity ratio‚Äù
epsilon: = (1 ‚Äì exp ( ‚Äì NTU * (1 ‚Äì C_R))) / (1 ‚Äì C_R * exp( ‚Äì NTU * (1 ‚Äì C_R))) 
‚ÄúEffectiveness of Counter-flow HX.‚Äù
Tc_2: = epsilon * (Th_1 ‚Äì Tc_1) + Tc_1‚Äú‚Ä¶finds Tc_2, exit temp of cold fluid‚Äù
Download free eBooks at bookboon.com

CRYOGENIC ENGINEERING: 
SOFTWARE SOLUTIONS VOL II A
228
Cryogenic liquefaction systems
228
Q: = C_c * (Tc_2 ‚Äì Tc_1) ‚ÄúW‚Ä¶heat transfer rate in the HX‚Äù
Th_2: = Th_1 ‚Äì Q / C_h ‚Äú‚Ä¶finds Th_2, exit temp of hot fluid‚Äù
	
ENDIF
IF C_c > C_h THEN
C_min: = C_h
C_max: = C_c
NTU: = (U * A) /C_min ‚Äú‚Ä¶finds NTU‚Äù
C_R: = C_min / C_max ‚Äú‚Ä¶finds capacity ratio‚Äù
epsilon: = (1 ‚Äì exp ( ‚Äì NTU * (1 ‚Äì C_R))) / (1 ‚Äì C_R * exp( ‚Äì NTU * (1 ‚Äì C_R))) 
‚ÄúEffectiveness of Counter-flow HX.‚Äù

CRYOGENIC ENGINEERING: 
SOFTWARE SOLUTIONS VOL II A
241
Cryogenic liquefaction systems
‚ÄúProb. 3.2.38. A Linde concentric tube HX is to be constructed from a 25 mm ID tube 
having a 2.5 mm wall thickness concentric to a 62.5 mm inside dia tube. Air at 10.13 MPa 
and 300 K enters the smaller tube, and air at 101.3 kPa and 85 K enters the larger tube 
and leaves at 289 K. The mass flow rate of high pressure stream is 0.25 kg/s, and the 
mass flow rate of low pressure stream is 0.233 kg/s. The mean sp. heat of high pressure 
stream is 1.56 kJ/kg.K, and the mean sp. heat of low pressure stream is 1.005 kJ/kg.K. If 
the concentric tube is wound in a 450 mm dia helix and heavily insulated, determine the 
length of concentric tubing required for this heat exchanger.[1]‚Äù
EES Solution:
‚ÄúFirst, let us solve this problem by LMTD method:‚Äù
‚ÄúData:‚Äù
d_i_h = 0.025 ‚Äúm‚Ä¶inside dia of high temp stream flow tube‚Äù
d_o_h = 0.03 ‚Äúm‚Ä¶outside dia of high temp stream flow tube‚Äù
d_i_c = 0.0625 ‚Äúm‚Ä¶inside dia of low temp stream flow tube‚Äù

CRYOGENIC ENGINEERING: 
SOFTWARE SOLUTIONS VOL II A
242
Cryogenic liquefaction systems
P1 = 10130 ‚ÄúkPa‚Ä¶high pressure flow‚Äù
P2 = 101.3 ‚ÄúkPa‚Ä¶Low pressure flow‚Äù
Th_1 = 300 ‚ÄúK‚Ä¶high pressure stream entry temp‚Äù
Tc_1 = 85 ‚ÄúK‚Ä¶low pressure stream entry temp‚Äù
Tc_2 = 289 ‚ÄúK‚Ä¶low pressure stream exit temp‚Äù
m_h = 0.25 ‚Äúkg/s‚Ä¶mass flow rate of high pressure stream‚Äù
m_c = 0.233 ‚Äúkg/s‚Ä¶mass flow rate of low pressure stream‚Äù
cp_h = 1560 ‚ÄúJ/kg.K‚Ä¶sp. heat of high pressure stream‚Äù
cp_c = 1005 ‚ÄúJ/kg.K‚Ä¶sp. heat of low pressure stream‚Äù
D_h = 0.45 ‚Äúm‚Ä¶helix dia‚Äù
‚ÄúCalculations:‚Äù
Q = m_c * cp_c * (Tc_2 ‚Äì Tc_1) ‚ÄúW‚Ä¶heat transfer‚Äú
Q = m_h * cp_h * (Th_1 ‚Äì Th_2) ‚ÄúK‚Ä¶determines exit temp of high pressure stream, Th_2‚Äù
T_avg_hot = (Th_1 + Th_2) / 2 ‚ÄúK‚Ä¶avg temp of hot flow‚Äù
T_avg_cold = (Tc_1 + Tc_2) / 2 ‚ÄúK‚Ä¶avg temp of cold flow‚Äù
‚ÄúTo find heat transfer coeff. h_h for high pressure stream‚Äù
A_i_h = (pi * d_i_h^2) / 4 ‚Äúm^2‚Ä¶cross-sectional area of high pressure flow‚Äù
G_h = m_h / A_i_h ‚Äúkg/s.m^2‚Ä¶mass velocity for high pressure flow‚Äù
mu_h = Viscosity(Air_ha,T=T_avg_hot,P=P1) ‚Äúkg/m.s‚Ä¶viscosity of high pressure flow‚Äù
rho_h = Density(Air_ha,T=T_avg_hot,P=P1) ‚Äúkg/m^3‚Ä¶density of high pressure flow‚Äù
Download free eBooks at bookboon.com

CRYOGENIC ENGINEERING: 
SOFTWARE SOLUTIONS VOL II A
243
Cryogenic liquefaction systems
243
N_Re_h = G_h * d_i_h / mu_h ‚Äú‚Ä¶Reynolds No. of high pressure stream = 683246‚Äù
N_Pr_h = Prandtl(Air_ha,T=T_avg_hot,P=P1) ‚Äú‚Ä¶Prandtl No. for high pressure stream‚Äù
‚ÄúThen, since Reynolds No. is > 3000, we have:‚Äù
j_H_h = (h_h / (cp_h * G_h)) * N_Pr_h^(2/3) ‚Äú‚Ä¶Colburn j-factor‚Äù
j_H_h = 0.023 * N_Re_h^(-0.2) * (1 + 3.5 * d_i_h / D_h) ‚Äú‚Ä¶determines h_h = W/m^2.K‚Äù
‚ÄúTo find heat transfer coeff. h_c for low pressure stream‚Äù
A_i_c = (pi / 4) * (d_i_c^2 ‚Äì d_o_h^2) ‚Äúm^2‚Ä¶cross-sectional area of low pressure flow‚Ä¶
the annulus‚Äù
G_c = m_c / A_i_c ‚Äúkg/s.m^2‚Ä¶mass velocity for low pressure flow‚Äù
mu_c = Viscosity(Air_ha,T=T_avg_cold,P=P2) ‚Äúkg/m.s‚Ä¶viscosity of high pressure flow‚Äù

Cryogenic liquefaction systems
rho_c = Density(Air_ha,T=T_avg_cold,P=P2) ‚Äúkg/m^3‚Ä¶density of low pressure flow‚Äù
N_Re_c = G_c * (d_i_c ‚Äì d_o_h) / mu_c ‚Äú‚Ä¶Reynolds No. of low pressure stream = 172104‚Äù
N_Pr_c = Prandtl(Air_ha,T=T_avg_cold,P=P2) ‚Äú‚Ä¶Prandtl No. for low pressure stream‚Äù
‚ÄúThen, since Reynolds No. is > 3000, we have:‚Äù
j_H_c = (h_c / (cp_c * G_c)) * N_Pr_c^(2/3) ‚Äú‚Ä¶Colburn j-factor‚Äù
j_H_c = 0.023 * N_Re_c^(-0.2) * (1 + 3.5 * (d_i_c ‚Äì d_o_h) / D_h) ‚Äú‚Ä¶determines h_c 
= W/m^2.K‚Äù
‚ÄúOverall heat transfer coeff. U:‚Äù
1/U = 1 / h_h + d_i_h / (h_c * d_o_h) ‚Äú‚Ä¶U based on heat transfer area on the high 
temp side‚Äù
‚ÄúTo find LMTD:‚Äù
DELTAT1 = (Th_1 ‚Äì Tc_2 ) ‚ÄúK‚Ä¶temp diff at the beginning of HX‚Äù
DELTAT2 = (Th_2 ‚Äì Tc_1 ) ‚ÄúK‚Ä¶temp diff at the end of HX‚Äù
LMTD = (DELTAT1 ‚Äì DELTAT2) / ln(DELTAT1 / DELTAT2) ‚ÄúK‚Ä¶calculates LMTD‚Äù
‚ÄúTo find the area and Length of HX:‚Äù
Q = U * Area_h * LMTD ‚Äú‚Ä¶finds area of heat transfer on the high temp side, m^2‚Äù
Area_h = pi * d_i_h * L ‚Äúm^2‚Ä¶area of heat transfer on the high (temp) pressure flow‚Ä¶
finds length of tube, L‚Äù

A: = 1 ‚Äì exp(-NTU * (1 ‚Äì C_R))

‚ÄúProb. 3.2.40. Write an EES Function to find the effectiveness of a Parallel-flow HX as a

for different values of Capacity ratios.‚Äù
EES Function:
FUNCTION ParallelflowHX_epsilon(NTU, C_R)
‚ÄúInputs: NTU‚Ä¶No. of Transfer Units = U A /C_min; Capacity ratio + C_R = C_min 
/ C_max)‚Äù
‚ÄúOutput: Effectiveness = epsilon‚Äù

A: = 1 ‚Äì exp(-NTU * (1 + C_R))
B: = 1 + C_R 
ParallelflowHX_epsilon: = A / B
ENDIF
END

‚ÄúProb. 3.2.43. Write EES Functions to find the exergy of heat supplied at constant temp, 
exergy of heat supplied at varying temp and at constant specific heat (cp), and the exergy 
of mass flow.‚Äù
EES Functions:
FUNCTION ExergyofHeat_ConstTemp(Q,T,T0)
{$ExergyofHeat_constTemp
This function returns the specific availability of a fluid in kJ/kg as a function of
Q[kJ], T [K], T0 (K)
}
ExergyofHeat_ConstTemp =Q * (1 ‚Äì T0 / T) ‚Äú‚Ä¶kJ/kg‚Äù
END
‚Äú=======================================================================‚Äù
FUNCTION ExergyofHeat_ConstPressure(cp,T,T0)
{$ExergyofHeat_constPressure
This function returns the specific availability of a fluid in kJ/kg as a function of
cp[kJ/kg.K], T [K], T0 (K)
}
ExergyofHeat_ConstPressure = cp * ( (T ‚Äì T0) ‚Äì T0 * ln(T / T0 )) ‚Äú‚Ä¶kJ/kg‚Äù
END
‚Äú======================================================================‚Äù
FUNCTION Exergy_massflow_IdealGas(IdealGas$,T, P, V, Z,T0, P0)
{$Exergy_massflow_IdealGas
Download free eBooks at bookboon.com

SOFTWARE SOLUTIONS VOL II A
260
Cryogenic liquefaction systems
This function returns the specific availability of IdealGas$ in kJ/kg as a function of
T [K], P [kPa], V [m/sec], Z [m], and ‚Äòdead state‚Äô P0 (kPa), T0 (K)
Ideal gases: Air, Ar, CO, CO2, N2, O2, H2, He, H2O, CH4 etc. 
See in EES: Options-Function Info-Fluid Props-Ideal gases.
}
IF ((T = T0) AND (P = P0)) THEN
Exergy_massflow_IdealGas: = 0
ELSE
g: = 9.81 ‚Äúm/s^2‚Äù
 h: = Enthalpy(IdealGas$, T=T) ‚Äú‚Ä¶kJ/kg‚Äù
 s: = Entropy(IdealGas$, T=T, P=P) ‚Äú‚Ä¶kJ/kg.K‚Äù
h0: = Enthalpy(IdealGas$, T=T0) ‚Äú‚Ä¶kJ/kg‚Äù
s0: = Entropy(IdealGas$, T=T0, P=P0) ‚Äú‚Ä¶kJ/kg.K‚Äù

‚Äú‚Ä¶kJ/kg‚Äù
ENDIF
END

FUNCTION Exergy_massflow_RealFluid(RealFluid$,T, P, V, Z,T0, P0)
{$Exergy_massflow_RealFluid
This function returns the specific availability of RealFluid$ in kJ/kg as a function of
T [K], P [kPa], V [m/sec], Z [m], and ‚Äòdead state‚Äô P0 (kPa), T0 (K)
RealFluids: Air_ha, Acetone, Ammonia, Argon, R12, R12, R124, R125, R134a, R23, R13, 
R22, Steam, Steam_NBS, Steam_IAPWS, Sulphur dioxide, Water, Xenon‚Ä¶etc.
See in EES: Options-Function Info-Fluid Props-Real Fluids.
}
IF ((T = T0) AND (P = P0)) THEN
Exergy_massflow_RealFluid: = 0
ELSE
g: = 9.81 ‚Äúm/s^2‚Äù
 h: = Enthalpy(RealFluid$, T=T, P=P) ‚Äú‚Ä¶kJ/kg‚Äù
 s: = Entropy(RealFluid$, T=T, P=P) ‚Äú‚Ä¶kJ/kg.K‚Äù
 h0: = Enthalpy(RealFluid$, T=T0, P=P0) ‚Äú‚Ä¶kJ/kg‚Äù

CRYOGENIC ENGINEERING: 
SOFTWARE SOLUTIONS VOL II A
265
Cryogenic liquefaction systems
Solution:
To determine the liquid yield, the work per unit mass compressed, the work per unit mass 
liquefied, and the figure of merit for a simple Linde-Hampson system. Then, determine the 
exergetic effcy of the cycle. Also, determine the exergy losses in the compressor (assuming 
the isothermal effcy of compressor as 70%), expansion valve and the heat exchanger.
‚ÄúData:‚Äù
Fluid$ = ‚ÄòNitrogen‚Äô
T_1=300 ‚Äú[K]‚Äù
T_2 = T_1 ‚ÄúK‚Äù
T0 = 300 ‚ÄúK‚Ä¶dead state‚Äù
P_1=101.3 ‚Äú[kPa]‚Äù
P0 =101.3 ‚ÄúkPa‚Ä¶dead state‚Äù
V = 0 ‚Äúm/s‚Äù
Z = 0 ‚Äúm‚Äù
P_2= 20000 ‚Äú[kPa]‚Äù
P_3 = P_2 ‚ÄúkPa‚Äù
P_4 = P_1 ‚ÄúkPa‚Äù
P_5 = P_1 ‚ÄúkPa‚Äù
P_6 = P_1 ‚ÄúkPa‚Äù
‚ÄúCalculations:‚Äù
s_1= Entropy(Fluid$,T=T_1,P=P_1) ‚Äú[kJ/kg-K]‚Äù
s_2= Entropy(Fluid$,P=P_2,T=T_2) ‚Äú[kJ/kg-K]‚Äù
Download free eBooks at bookboon.com

CRYOGENIC ENGINEERING: 
SOFTWARE SOLUTIONS VOL II A
266
Cryogenic liquefaction systems
h_1= Enthalpy(Fluid$,P=P_1,T=T_1) ‚Äú[kJ/kg]‚Äù
h_2= Enthalpy(Fluid$,P=P_2,T=T_2) ‚Äú[kJ/kg]‚Äù
h_f = Enthalpy(Fluid$,x=0,P=P_1) ‚Äú[kJ/kg]‚Äù
h_6 = h_f ‚Äú‚Ä¶sat. liquid enthalpy‚Äù
y = (h_1 ‚Äì h_2) / (h_1 ‚Äì h_f) ‚Äú‚Ä¶fraction liquefied‚Äù
(h_2 ‚Äì h_3) = (1 ‚Äì y) * (h_1 ‚Äì h_5) ‚Äú‚Ä¶finds h_3‚Äù
h_5 = Enthalpy(Fluid$, x=1,P=P_5) ‚Äú[kJ/kg]‚Äù
h_4 = h_3 ‚Äú‚Ä¶since process 3‚Äì4 in the J-T valve is isenthalpic‚Äù
s_3 = Entropy(Fluid$,P=P_3,h=h_3) ‚Äú[kJ/kg-K]‚Äù
s_4 = Entropy(Fluid$,P=P_4,h=h_4) ‚Äú[kJ/kg-K]‚Äù
s_5= Entropy(Fluid$,P=P_5,h=h_5) ‚Äú[kJ/kg-K]‚Äù
s_6 = Entropy(Fluid$,P=P_6,h=h_6) ‚Äú[kJ/kg-K]‚Äù
T_3=Temperature(Fluid$,P=P_3,s=s_3) ‚ÄúK‚Ä¶temp at point 3‚Äù
T_6=Temperature(Fluid$,P=P_6,x = 0) ‚ÄúK‚Ä¶temp at point 6‚Äù
T_4=T_6 ‚ÄúK‚Ä¶temp at point 4‚Äù
T_5=T_6 ‚ÄúK‚Ä¶temp at point 5‚Äù
Wperkggas =T_1 * (s_1 ‚Äì s_2) ‚Äì (h_1 ‚Äì h_2) ‚Äú[kJ/kg]‚Äù
Wperkgliq = (T_1 * (s_1 ‚Äì s_2 )- (h_1 ‚Äì h_2)) / y ‚Äú[kJ/kg]‚Äù 
W_ideal = Ideal_Work(FLUID$, P_1,T_1) ‚ÄúkJ/kg‚Ä¶ideal work of liquefaction‚Äù
FOM = W_ideal / Wperkgliq ‚Äú‚Ä¶Figure of Merit‚Äù
COP = (h_1 ‚Äì h_2) / Wperkggas ‚Äú‚Ä¶Coeff of Performance‚Äù
Download free eBooks at bookboon.com

‚ÄúExergies:‚Äù

 s0 = Entropy(Fluid$, T=T0, P=P0) ‚Äú‚Ä¶kJ/kg.K‚Äù
ef_1 = (h_1 ‚Äì h0) ‚Äì T0 * (s_1 ‚Äì s0) ‚ÄúkJ/kg‚Ä¶exergy of flow at point 1‚Äù
ef_2 = (h_2 ‚Äì h0) ‚Äì T0 * (s_2 ‚Äì s0) ‚ÄúkJ/kg‚Ä¶exergy of flow at point 2‚Äù
ef_3 = (h_3 ‚Äì h0) ‚Äì T0 * (s_3 ‚Äì s0) ‚ÄúkJ/kg‚Ä¶exergy of flow at point 3‚Äù
ef_4 = (h_4 ‚Äì h0) ‚Äì T0 * (s_4 ‚Äì s0) ‚ÄúkJ/kg‚Ä¶exergy of flow at point 4‚Äù
ef_5 = (h_5 ‚Äì h0) ‚Äì T0 * (s_5 ‚Äì s0) ‚ÄúkJ/kg‚Ä¶exergy of flow at point 5‚Äù
ef_6 = (h_6 ‚Äì h0) ‚Äì T0 * (s_6 ‚Äì s0) ‚ÄúkJ/kg‚Ä¶exergy of flow at point 6‚Äù

‚ÄúExergetic effcy.:‚Äù
W_rev = ef_6 ‚Äì ef_1 ‚Äú‚Ä¶reversible work, kJ/kg‚Äù
W_actual = Wperkgliq ‚Äú‚Ä¶kJ/kg liq‚Ä¶actual work input‚Äù
W_actual = W_rev + exergy_destroyed ‚Äú‚Ä¶finds exergy destroyed, kJ/kg of liq.‚Äù
eta_II_cycle = W_rev / W_actual ‚Äú‚Ä¶Second Law effcy of cycle = exergetic effcy.‚Äù
‚ÄúWriting exergy balance to determine exergy losses in each component:‚Äù
‚ÄúExergy loss in Isothermal compressor:‚Äù
‚ÄúNote that in an isothermal compressor, heat is rejected; however, in this case its exergy is 
zero since heat is rejected at ambient conditions.‚Äù
‚ÄúWriting an exergy balance for a compressor of isothermal effcy = 70%:‚Äù
ef_1 + Wperkggas/0.7 = ef_2 + DELTAe_comp ‚Äú‚Ä¶finds exergy loss in compressor, 
DELTAe_comp‚Äù
‚ÄúExergy loss in Heat exchanger:‚Äù
‚ÄúWriting an exergy balance:‚Äù
ef_2 +(1 ‚Äì y) * ef_5 = ef_3 + (1 ‚Äì y) * ef_1 + DELTAe_HX ‚Äú‚Ä¶finds exergy loss in HX, 
DELTAe_HX‚Äù
‚ÄúExergy loss in Expansion valve:‚Äù
‚ÄúWriting an exergy balance:‚Äù
ef_3 = ef_4 + DELTAe_EV ‚Äú‚Ä¶finds exergy loss in Expn valve, DELTAe_EV‚Äù

Prob. 3.2.45. Data for an ideal Kapitza system is given below:
Nitrogen is the working fluid, the system operates from 101.3 kPa (1 atm) and 300 K to 
5.07 MPa (i.e. 50 atm.). At 5.07 MPa and 255 K, 40% of the main flow is diverted to 
the reversible adiabatic expander. Remainder flows through the heat exchanger and expands 
through the expansion valve to 101.3 kPa. Determine the liquid yield and the work per 
unit mass liquefied, assuming that the expander work is utilized in the compression process. 
Make a second law analysis of the system.
Solution:
‚ÄúNote that Kapitza system is similar to Claude system except that the last heat exchanger (i.e. 
low temp heat exchanger) is eliminated and the first heat exchanger consists of regenerators.
For calculation purposes, the formulas are the same as for Claude system.‚Äù

P0 = 101.3 ‚ÄúkPa‚Ä¶Ambient or dead state‚Äù
T0 = 300 ‚ÄúK‚Ä¶Ambient or dead state‚Äù
V = 0 ‚Äúm/s‚Äù
Z = 0 ‚Äúm‚Äù
x = 0.4 ‚Äú‚Ä¶expander flow rate ratio‚Äù
s[1]=Entropy(Fluid$,T=T[1],P=P[1]) ‚Äú[kJ/kg-K]‚Äù
s[2]=Entropy(Fluid$,P=P[2],T=T[2]) ‚Äú[kJ/kg-K]‚Äù
s[3]=Entropy(Fluid$,P=P[3],T=T[3]) ‚Äú[kJ/kg-K]‚Äù
s[8] = s[3] ‚Äú‚Ä¶entropy after isentropic expansion in expander‚Äù
h[1]=Enthalpy(Fluid$,T=T[1],P=P[1]) ‚Äú[kJ/kg]‚Äù
h[2]=Enthalpy(Fluid$,P=P[2],T=T[2]) ‚Äú[kJ/kg]‚Äù
h[3]=Enthalpy(Fluid$,P=P[3],T=T[3]) ‚Äú[kJ/kg]‚Äù
h[8] = Enthalpy(Fluid$,P=P[8],s= s[8]) ‚Äú[kJ/kg]‚Äù
h[9]=Enthalpy(Fluid$,x=1,P=P[9]) ‚Äú[kJ/kg]‚Äù
h[10] = Enthalpy(Fluid$,x=0,P=P[10]) ‚Äú[kJ/kg]‚Äù
‚ÄúCalculations:‚Äù
y = (h[1] ‚Äì h[2]) / (h[1] ‚Äì h[10]) + x * ((h[3] ‚Äì h[8]) / (h[1] ‚Äì h[10])) ‚Äú‚Ä¶fraction liquefied‚Äù
W_exp_perkggas = x * (h[3] ‚Äì h[8]) ‚ÄúkJ/kg of gas compressed‚Äù
W_net_perkggas =T[1] * (s[1] ‚Äì s[2]) ‚Äì (h[1] ‚Äì h[2]) ‚Äì x * (h[3] ‚Äì h[8]) ‚Äú[kJ/kg]‚Ä¶net 
work per kg gas compressed..when the expander work is utilised in compression‚Äù
W_net_perkgliq = W_net_perkggas / y ‚Äú[kJ/kg]‚Ä¶net work reqd per kg liq.‚Äù 
W_ideal = Ideal_Work(FLUID$, P[1],T[1]) ‚ÄúkJ/kg‚Ä¶ideal work of liquefaction‚Äù

FOM = W_ideal / W_net_perkgliq ‚Äú‚Ä¶Figure of Merit‚Äù
COP = (h[1] ‚Äì h[2]) / W_net_perkggas ‚Äú‚Ä¶Coeff of Performance‚Äù
‚Äú
‚Äù
T[9]=Temperature(Fluid$,P=P[9],x = 1) ‚ÄúK‚Ä¶temp at point 9‚Ä¶sat. liq.‚Äù
T[5] = T[10]
T[9] = T[10]
(1 ‚Äì x) * h[5] = y * h[10] + (1 ‚Äì y) * h[9] ‚Äú‚Ä¶finds h[5]‚Ä¶ kJ/kg‚Äù
h[4] = h[5] ‚Äú‚Ä¶for expansion in the Expansion Valve‚Äù
T[4] = Temperature(Fluid$,P=P[4],h = h[4]) ‚ÄúK‚Ä¶temp at point 4‚Äù
T[8] = Temperature(Fluid$,P=P[8],h = h[8]) ‚ÄúK‚Ä¶temp at point 8‚Äù
T[6] = Temperature(Fluid$,P=P[6],h = h[6]) ‚ÄúK‚Ä¶temp at point 6‚Äù
x * h[8] + (1 ‚Äì x ‚Äì y) * h[9] = (1 ‚Äì y) * h[6] ‚Äú‚Ä¶finds h[6]‚Ä¶by energy balance for mixing‚Äù
(1 ‚Äì x) * (h[3] ‚Äì h[4]) = (1 ‚Äì y) * (h[7] ‚Äì h[6]) ‚Äú‚Ä¶finds h[7]‚Ä¶ by energy balance on HX2‚Äù
T[7] = Temperature(Fluid$,P=P[7],h = h[7]) ‚ÄúK‚Ä¶temp at point 7‚Äù
h[2] ‚Äì h[3] = (1 ‚Äì y) * (h[11] ‚Äì h[7]) ‚Äú‚Ä¶finds h[11]‚Ä¶by energy balance on HX1‚Äù
T[11] = Temperature(Fluid$,P=P[11],h = h[11]) ‚ÄúK‚Ä¶temp at point 11‚Äù
s[4]=Entropy(Fluid$,T=T[4],P=P[4]) ‚Äú[kJ/kg-K]‚Äù
s[5]=Entropy(Fluid$,P=P[5],x= (1 ‚Äì y)) ‚Äú[kJ/kg-K]‚Äù
s[6]=Entropy(Fluid$,P=P[6],T=T[6]) ‚Äú[kJ/kg-K]‚Äù
s[7]=Entropy(Fluid$,T=T[7],P=P[7]) ‚Äú[kJ/kg-K]‚Äù
s[9]=Entropy(Fluid$,P=P[9], x = 1) ‚Äú[kJ/kg-K]‚Äù

‚ÄúTo find exergies:‚Äù
h0 = Enthalpy(Fluid$, T=T0, P=P0) ‚Äú‚Ä¶kJ/kg‚Äù
s0 = Entropy(Fluid$, T=T0, P=P0) ‚Äú‚Ä¶kJ/kg.K‚Äù
duplicate k = 1,11
ex[k] = (h[k] ‚Äì h0) ‚Äì T0 * (s[k] ‚Äì s0) +( V^2 / 2) / 1000 + (9.81 * Z) / 1000 ‚Äú‚Ä¶.kJ/kg‚Äù
End
‚ÄúExergetic or Second Law effcy.:‚Äù
W_rev = ex[10] ‚Äì ex[1] ‚Äú‚Ä¶reversible work, kJ/kg‚Äù

W_actual = W_rev + exergy_destroyed ‚Äú‚Ä¶finds exergy destroyed, kJ/kg of liq‚Äù
eta_II_cycle = W_rev / W_actual ‚Äú‚Ä¶Second Law effcy of cycle = exergetic effcy.‚Äù
‚ÄúWriting exergy balance to determine exergy losses in each component:‚Äù
‚ÄúExergy loss in Isothermal compressor:‚Äù
‚ÄúNote that in an isothermal compressor, heat is rejected; however, in this case its exergy is 
zero since heat is rejected at ambient conditions.‚Äù
‚ÄúWriting an exergy balance for a compressor of isothermal effcy = 70%:‚Äù
ex[1] + (T[1] * (s[1] ‚Äì s[2]) ‚Äì (h[1] ‚Äì h[2]))/0.7 = ex[2] + DELTAe_comp ‚Äú‚Ä¶finds exergy 
loss in compressor, DELTAe_comp‚Äù
‚ÄúExergy loss in Heat exchanger ‚Äì 1:‚Äù
‚ÄúWriting an exergy balance:‚Äù
ex[2] +(1 ‚Äì y) * ex[7] = ex[3] + (1 ‚Äì y) * ex[11] + DELTAe_HX1 ‚Äú‚Ä¶finds exergy loss in 
HX-1‚Ä¶DELTAe_HX1‚Äù
‚ÄúExergy loss in Heat exchanger ‚Äì 2:‚Äù
‚ÄúWriting an exergy balance:‚Äù
(1 ‚Äì x) * ex[3] + (1 ‚Äì y) * ex[6]= (1 ‚Äì x) * ex[4] + (1 ‚Äì y) * ex[7] + DELTAe_HX2  
‚Äú‚Ä¶finds exergy loss in HX-2‚Ä¶DELTAe_HX2‚Äù
‚ÄúExergy loss in Expansion valve:‚Äù
‚ÄúWriting an exergy balance:‚Äù
(1 ‚Äì x) * ex[4] = (1 ‚Äì x) * ex[5]+ DELTAe_EV ‚Äú‚Ä¶finds exergy loss in Expn valve, 
DELTAe_EV‚Äù
